But if a group of people design a new type of computer, you will say a priori that computers will never be able to do what the baby can do. Why? What is it in that baby that persuades you?

R. Sheldrake: You see, there are a lot of things which we recognize and understand directly without being able to put everything into explicitly stored-up recognition programmes. I can recognize many different kinds of flowers, trees and animals. If I have to say how I recognize them, what is it that makes me recognize them, it will be very difficult for me to tell you. I think it will be difficult for you, too.

Krishnamurti: But, sir, when you recognize, it is based on memory.

A. Chandmal: They are working on pattern recognition. There is tremendous research going into it today. Computers are beginning to recognize some things visually.

R. Sheldrake: But there is a certain intuitive sense.

A. Chandmal: What is intuition?

R. Sheldrake: It is notoriously difficult to say what intuition is.

A. Chandmal: It is just a word. Unless you know what it means, you cannot use that word.

R. Sheldrake: No. You don’t have to be able to spell out in mathematical formula whatever words mean.

A. Chandmal: Spell it out in words. What is intuition?

R. Sheldrake: Intuition is grasping something more, seeing something more, insight into something which involves a direct kind of knowledge which does not have to go through a process of words, thought and action.

A. Chandmal: How do you know it has not gone through the process of word or thought? It could have done it subconsciously in your mind, the brain has been working on it, and it emerges instantaneously, and you call it intuition. It does not mean that it has not gone through the process of thought.

R. Sheldrake: It may have gone through such processes. If, for everything I say, you are going to postulate hidden processes...

A. Chandmal: I am not postulating.

R. Sheldrake: Yes, you are.

P. Jayakar: Sir, the problem seems to be that if the brain is a closed circuit only, then what Asit says is true. But the ‘but’ comes in because the whole reason for our being here is, can there be an acceleration of the very capacity of the brain so that it ceases to be a process? Is the brain a closed circuit?

R. Sheldrake: The trouble is, it takes a long time to answer these questions. I have my own theory about biology which would deny most of these basic premises. You see, the conventional theory of biology, including the conventional theory of the brain, starts from the assumption that there are simply mechanical, chemical or physical processes within the organism. Now, only 99 per cent of biology is based on this assumption, and therefore, the kind of language in which we speak is based on that kind of thinking.

I disagree with the assumption, firstly, that the brain is a closed circuit. Secondly, that it works entirely mechanically or chemically or electrically and so on. So, I think we have a theory of life which says that living organisms are nothing but machines, and then we have a theory which says it has nothing to do with machines. Why can’t we model them by machines? This is the basis of your argument, and it seems quite reasonable on the face of it, but there are a number of assumptions.

P. Jayakar: He posited three things: Whether the brain as it is today is a closed circuit; what is intelligence; and what creativity is.

A. Chandmal: I didn’t say the brain was a closed circuit.

Krishnamurti: May I ask a question, sir? Would you consider that the brain has infinite capacity? Don’t say ‘no’ right away. Let us use the word ‘capacity’. I don’t like the word ‘capacity’ because for us capacity is educated knowledge and all that. But if I can use that word, the brain has infinite capacity. Look what it has done in the technological world, including the computer.

A. Chandmal: You can’t say that thought is limited and then say that the brain has infinite capacity.

Krishnamurti: Yes, I am going to come to that. Thought has limited the brain, has conditioned the brain. Would you agree? I am a Hindu, I believe in all the superstitions, all the nonsense. Right?

A. Chandmal: You are separating thought and brain.

Krishnamurti: No, no. I want to find out if the brain can ever be free from its own limitation, thought, knowledge, emotion. All right, call it thought. Can the brain which has been conditioned by thought, if that conditioning is somehow freed, it has got...

A. Chandmal: You can’t say that.

Krishnamurti: It may. You are understanding now? You have been to the moon, the brain has created cruise missiles, it has had extraordinary technological movement. Agreed? Now, is there an instrument which is not thought? This is not romantic speculation. I am just asking; I am not saying there is or there is not. You understand my question? Thought is a worn-out instrument. I think it has reached its limit, tether, because it has not solved the human problem. So, is there a way of looking which is not thought but which can instead of going out there, going to the heavens and all that, turn inwards? That inward movement is the infinite.

R. Ravindra: Still it has not solved the human problem.

Krishnamurti: I am going to show it will. No, thought will not solve the human problems. Either it is a fact or it is not a fact. On the contrary, it is increasing human problems. Right?

Questioner: Your question is: Is there anything other than thought which could be an instrument?

Krishnamurti: Yes, you may not agree with what I am going to say presently. Then, perhaps, that instrument can look both outward and inward, and that is infinite.

Questioner: Psychologists try to discover what is within; at least they profess to do this.

Krishnamurti: I know, sir, what they say is all mechanical.

Questioner: I accept what you say.

Krishnamurti: Don’t accept, sir. I hesitate to accept what I say too. I want first to be quite clear that thought has not solved human problems. It has solved technological, not human problems—my relationship with my wife, my relationship with the community, my relationship with the heavens, and all the rest of it. And thought tries to resolve these problems and it has made things worse. It is so obvious. So I am now asking, is there something which is not thought, which is not mechanical?

A. Chandmal: You are asking in other words what Pupulji was asking the other day: Is there a sensory perception without thought?

Krishnamurti: Yes. Will you listen to something? Life is a movement, going out and coming in, like the tide. I create the world, and the world then controls me. And I react to the world. It is movement. Would you agree to that? Now, if you see the same thing as I see—not that you must—it is a movement out and in, this is our life, action and reaction, reward and punishment. Can this movement stop?

P. Jayakar: You have to move out of your closed circuit of the computer to even face that question.

Krishnamurti: No, not move out of the circuit. This is our life. Now, as long as this movement exists, I am caught in time, that is evolution.

R. Sheldrake: Why not just say that is life, evolution?

Krishnamurti: Yes, and that is: I am evolving. This movement gets better, worse, it is always movement. So, as long as this movement exists, I am mechanical.

Questioner: Only mechanical?

Krishnamurti: Yes, I see a woman and I want her. I see a garden, I want it. It is action and reaction, reward and punishment, punishment and reward. Where is intelligence in that? As long as you are caught in that, your intelligence is out; it is a mechanical intelligence: You hate me and I hate you back.

A. Chandmal: I follow that.

Krishnamurti: If you accept that, intelligence is something totally different from thought.

R. Sheldrake: If what you are saying is what I think it is, perhaps you could say it is cause and effect, action and reaction, instead of ‘mechanical’.

Krishnamurti: Yes, yes.

R. Sheldrake: Now there is a certain kind of low level activity, what people ordinarily call intelligence, which perhaps we can better call ingenuity, where, in order to get something you want—but you may not be able to get it in a straightforward way—you may have to resort to some fairly original way, some new kind of competence, making some bogus documents and so on. There is a certain kind of ingenuity which is not purely mechanical. It is subsumed down to a certain mechanical set of desires and within that is the framework of certain inventiveness. So the framework may be one of action-reaction but within that we exhibit considerable ingenuity and inventiveness.

Krishnamurti: I would not call that intelligence.

R. Sheldrake: No. But in ordinary language it is often called intelligence. An intelligent businessman is one who would think of ways of getting more of what he wants.

Krishnamurti: Yes. I would not call that intelligence.

R. Sheldrake: I would call it ingenuity or inventiveness.

Krishnamurti: Call it inventiveness. I won’t call it intuition because that is a different thing.

R. Sheldrake: No, ingenuity.

Krishnamurti: To be ingenious is solving problems of god, problems of heaven, problems of painting, etc. It is within the same area, in the same field. I may move from one corner to the other corner of the same field and I call that ingenuity and I say all that has nothing to do with intelligence. Intelligence is something totally different.

Questioner: Will you elaborate on what we call intelligence?

Krishnamurti: I don’t want to elaborate. Ingenuity, choice, cleverness, moving from one point to another, from one corner to another but within the same field, that is what we are doing.

P. Jayakar: That is the field of the known.

Krishnamurti: Yes, yes. I don’t want to use that word for the moment.

A. Chandmal: I was just wondering why we have evolved like that.

Krishnamurti: It is essentially based on reward and punishment.

A. Chandmal: But I am asking what is the reason in particular that we have evolved like that?

Krishnamurti: What was the cause of it?

A. Chandmal: It must have had tremendous advantage.

Krishnamurti: Of course, it is completely secure. Secure in the sense, at least for the time being, but the time being creates wars. So we don’t have to elaborate. Would you go along up to this point that this is not intelligence?

A. Chandmal: Yes.

Krishnamurti: Right. Then let us enquire what is intelligence. If this is not a theory, if it is out of my system, that means the movement of reaction has stopped, and that is the movement of time. Agreed?

A. Chandmal: When you say time, I don’t understand.

Krishnamurti: Time in the sense I have evolved in this process.

Questioner: That is the movement of life.

Krishnamurti: Yes. And that is unintelligence. Therefore, don’t call it intelligence. So, what is intelligence? As long as I am in this field there is no intelligence; it is adaptability.

A. Chandmal: But one has to respond.

Krishnamurti: We will find out. If this is not intelligence, then we have to go into something quite different. Agreed? If I totally deny, not verbally but actually, this is not intelligence, then what happens to the mind which has been caught in this? Do you understand my question? As long as we are functioning in time, cause, effect, action, reaction, which is this movement of the tide going out and coming in, as long as my whole attitude to life is that and I refuse to move out of that, there is nothing to be said. But if I see that, that will not solve the problems of humanity; then I have to look in another direction.

P. Jayakar: What is this looking?

Krishnamurti: My eyes have always been seeing in this direction only. And you come along and tell me, look in other directions. I can’t because my eyesight has been so conditioned that I don’t even turn round to look. So I must be first free of this. I can’t look in any other direction if I am not free of this.

P. Jayakar: I want to ask you a question. Can I look at my own instrument? Can perception look at its own instrument? Can perception, which is a flow, see itself?

Krishnamurti: Don’t call it an instrument.

P. Jayakar: A faculty.

Krishnamurti: No, I won’t even call it a faculty.

P. Jayakar: Can perception perceive itself?

Krishnamurti: Can perception see itself as perceiving? Perception seeing itself in action, in seeing itself a perception.

P. Jayakar: Don’t bring in action.

Krishnamurti: Perception seeing itself perceiving—then it is not perception.

P. Jayakar: You see, you posed a question which is totally unanswerable—that this movement, which is moving, reflects the movement... can I see the falsity of it and end it? I have always thought that a wrong question. It can never see that because perception is self-contained.

Krishnamurti: Would you say this movement is the wandering of desire?

P. Jayakar: Yes. This movement is the wandering of desire.

Krishnamurti: Can this desire be seen as a whole, not the object of desire, but desire itself? Can it see itself as a movement of attraction?

P. Jayakar: Instead, even without bringing in attraction, can desire see itself?

Krishnamurti: To understand if desire can see itself, one must go into desire. Desire exists only when thought comes into sensation.

A. Chandmal: This question is very important. We are operating in that field. Anything operating in that field...

P. Jayakar: Can never deny that field.

Krishnamurti: Of course. There is this movement. As long as I am in that movement, you cannot ask me to see it as the false and deny it.

P. Jayakar: Therefore, where do I look?

Krishnamurti: You don’t have to look. The thing is, stop this movement. Find out, discover for yourself how to end this movement. Is that possible at all?

P. Jayakar: I think it is possible to cut.

Krishnamurti: Be careful when you use the word ‘cut’. Who is the cutter?

P. Jayakar: Without the cutter.

Krishnamurti: Therefore, what does that mean? Go on. Don’t complicate the issue. Just see who is the cutter. There is no cutter. Then what happens? If there is no entity who can cut, stop, then...

P. Jayakar: It is just perceiving.

Krishnamurti: That is all. There is only perceiving. There is not the perceiver perceiving nor the perceiver investigating what he is perceiving. There is only perception, right? Perception of that which is false.

P. Jayakar: The perceiving throws light on the false. There is only perceiving.

Krishnamurti: There is only perceiving. Stick to that. Then we will enquire into what is perceiving. What is perception without the word, without the name, without remembrances, perceiving something which one calls intuition? I don’t like to use that word, forgive me. Perception is direct insight.

P. Jayakar: Is the question one of being completely awake?

Krishnamurti: Would you call that attention?

P. Jayakar: To be completely awake is attention.

Krishnamurti: That is all.

P. Jayakar: That the computer can never do.

Krishnamurti: Asit is taking it in, he is not answering. Sir, is there an end to thought? Time must have a stop, right?

A. Chandmal: I understand.

R. Ravindra: Can I ask you a question: What happens when we perceive with insight?

Krishnamurti: There is this perception of insight and the brain cells themselves change. Can your thought ever stop when your brain has been conditioned in time, in this movement... cause, effect, action, reaction and all that suddenly stops? Hasn’t the brain undergone a radical change? Of course it has.

R. Ravindra: I have to ask you this question again. If there is such a seeing that the brain cells change, what happens after perceiving it?

A. Chandmal: Only the physical brain has changed and I am afraid it dies.

Krishnamurti: That is why we are going into the question of consciousness.

A. Chandmal: Does this end with death? Then all that will be different from the computer...

Krishnamurti: Sir, how will you translate all this to your friends who are computer experts?

A. Chandmal: They are going to continue doing what they are doing—trying to produce super-computers.

P. Jayakar: The question then comes in. How can man so accelerate the other to bring into being this new perception?

A. Chandmal: One can only see this movement and do nothing else.

Krishnamurti: That is all.

Madras, 31st December 1982
